					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Japanese noodle dish</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Lamian" title="Lamian">Lamian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Instant_noodles" title="Instant noodles">Instant noodles</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ramune" title="Ramune">Ramune</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Japanese noodle dish. For the instant version and other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Ramen_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Ramen (disambiguation)">Ramen (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

especially regional, with various ingredients and toppings</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.25em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1316373843">@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .sister-inline-image img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .sister-inline-image img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}</style><span class="noviewer sister-inline-image" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/20px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a></span> <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Ramen" class="extiw" title="b:Cookbook:Ramen">Cookbook: Ramen</a></li><li>&#160;<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1316373843" /><span class="noviewer sister-inline-image" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/20px-Commons-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="12" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/40px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1376" /></a></span> <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Ramen" class="extiw" title="commons:Category:Ramen">Media&#58; Ramen</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Ramen</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">拉麺, ラーメン or らあめん</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">rāmen</i></span>; <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="ja-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">&#91;ɾaꜜːmeɴ&#93;</a></span> <span class="ext-phonos"><span data-nosnippet="" id="ooui-php-1" class="noexcerpt ext-phonos-PhonosButton ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-buttonElement oo-ui-buttonElement-frameless oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-buttonWidget" data-ooui="{&quot;_&quot;:&quot;mw.Phonos.PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;\/\/upload.wikimedia.org\/wikipedia\/commons\/transcoded\/6\/6d\/TomJ-Ramen.ogg\/TomJ-Ramen.ogg.mp3&quot;,&quot;rel&quot;:[&quot;nofollow&quot;],&quot;framed&quot;:false,&quot;icon&quot;:&quot;volumeUp&quot;,&quot;data&quot;:{&quot;ipa&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;lang&quot;:&quot;en&quot;,&quot;wikibase&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;file&quot;:&quot;TomJ-Ramen.ogg&quot;},&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;noexcerpt&quot;,&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel&quot;]}"><a role="button" tabindex="0" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/6d/TomJ-Ramen.ogg/TomJ-Ramen.ogg.mp3" rel="nofollow" aria-label="Play audio" title="Play audio" class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label"></span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:TomJ-Ramen.ogg" title="File:TomJ-Ramen.ogg">ⓘ</a></sup></span>)</span> is a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Noodle" title="Noodle">noodle</a> dish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cwiertka&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cwiertka-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is a part of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Chinese_cuisine" title="Japanese Chinese cuisine">Japanese Chinese cuisine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:11&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It includes <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_noodles" title="Alkaline noodles">Chinese-style alkaline wheat noodles</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">中華麺</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">chūkamen</i></span>)</span> served in several flavors of hot <a href="/wiki/Broth" title="Broth">broth</a>. Common flavors are <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a>, with typical toppings including sliced pork<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Char_siu" title="Char siu">chāshū</a></i></span>)</span>, <a href="/wiki/Nori" title="Nori">nori</a> (dried seaweed), lacto-fermented bamboo shoots (<i><a href="/wiki/Menma" title="Menma">menma</a></i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Narutomaki" title="Narutomaki">narutomaki</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Scallion" title="Scallion">scallions</a>. Nearly every region in Japan has its own variation of ramen, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Tonkotsu" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonkotsu">tonkotsu</a></i> (pork bone broth) ramen of <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a> and the <i>miso</i> ramen of <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>.
</p><p>The origins of ramen can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Yokohama_Chinatown" title="Yokohama Chinatown">Yokohama Chinatown</a> in the late 19th century. While the word "ramen" is a Japanese borrowing of the Chinese word <i><a href="/wiki/Lamian" title="Lamian">lāmiàn</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">拉麵</span></span>), meaning "pulled noodles", the ramen does not actually derive from any lamian dishes. Lamian is a part of northern Chinese cuisine, whereas the ramen evolved from southern Chinese noodle dishes from regions such as <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, reflecting the demographics of Chinese immigrants in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ramen was largely confined to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Japan" title="Chinese people in Japan">Chinese community in Japan</a> and was never popular nationwide until after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> (specifically the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a>), following increased wheat consumption due to rice shortages and the return of millions of Japanese colonial settlers from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. In 1958, <a href="/wiki/Instant_noodles" title="Instant noodles">instant noodles</a> were invented by <a href="/wiki/Momofuku_Ando" title="Momofuku Ando">Momofuku Ando</a>, further popularizing the dish.
</p><p>Ramen was originally looked down upon by the Japanese due to <a href="/wiki/Anti-Chinese_sentiment_in_Japan" title="Anti-Chinese sentiment in Japan">racial discrimination against the Chinese</a> and its status as an inexpensive food associated with the <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today, ramen is considered a <a href="/wiki/National_dish" title="National dish">national dish</a> of Japan, with many regional varieties and a wide range of toppings. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Sapporo" title="Sapporo">Sapporo</a>'s rich miso ramen, <a href="/wiki/Hakodate" title="Hakodate">Hakodate</a>'s salt-flavored ramen, <a href="/wiki/Kitakata,_Fukushima" title="Kitakata, Fukushima">Kitakata</a>'s thick, flat noodles in pork-and-<a href="/wiki/Niboshi" title="Niboshi">niboshi</a> broth, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>-style ramen with soy-flavored chicken broth, <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Iekei_ramen" title="Iekei ramen">Iekei ramen</a> with soy-flavored pork broth, <a href="/wiki/Wakayama_(city)" title="Wakayama (city)">Wakayama</a>'s soy sauce and pork bone broth, and <a href="/wiki/Hakata" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakata">Hakata</a>'s milky <i>tonkotsu</i> (pork bone) broth. Ramen is offered in various establishments and locations, with the best quality usually found in specialist ramen shops called <i>rāmen'ya</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ラーメン屋</span></span>).
</p><p>Ramen's popularity has spread outside of Japan, becoming a cultural icon representing the country worldwide. In <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, ramen is known both by its original name "ramen" (<span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">라멘</span></span>) as well as <i>ramyeon</i> (<span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hang">라면</span></span>), a local variation on the dish. In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, ramen is called <i>rìshì lāmiàn</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">日式拉面</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">日式拉麵</span></span> "Japanese-style lamian"). Ramen has also made its way into Western restaurant chains. Instant ramen was exported from Japan in 1971 and has since gained international recognition. The global popularity of ramen has sometimes led to the term being misused in the <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">Anglosphere</a> as a catch-all for any <a href="/wiki/Noodle_soup" title="Noodle soup">noodle soup</a> dish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:11&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ramen_1928.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="From Seiichi Yoshida, How to Prepare Delicious and Economical Chinese Dishes (1928)." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ramen_1928.jpg/250px-Ramen_1928.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="288" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ramen_1928.jpg/500px-Ramen_1928.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="656" data-file-height="755" /></a><figcaption>From Seiichi Yoshida, <i>How to Prepare Delicious and Economical Chinese Dishes</i> (1928)</figcaption></figure>
<p>The word <i>ramen</i> is a Japanese borrowing of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Lamian" title="Lamian">lamian</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">拉麵</span></span>, 'pulled noodles').<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ramanfor&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ramanfor-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A common misconception is that ramen is a Japanese adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Lamian" title="Lamian">lamian</a>, but the two dishes have no direct relation, and how ramen came to adopt its name from lamian remains unclear.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ramen evolved from southern Chinese noodle dishes, primarily <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_cuisine" title="Cantonese cuisine">Cantonese</a>, as opposed to northern Chinese noodle dishes that may feature lamian.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The word <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">ramen</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">拉麺</span></span>)</span> first appeared in Japan in Seiichi Yoshida's <i>How to Prepare Delicious and Economical Chinese Dishes</i> (1928). In the book, Yoshida describes how to make <i>ramen</i> using flour and <i><a href="/wiki/Kansui" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansui">kansui</a></i>, kneading it by hand, and stretching it with an illustration. He also states that <i>ramen</i> is better suited for soup or cold noodles than for baked noodles. In this case, however, <i>ramen</i> refers to actual lamian (hand-pulled noodles), not the noodle soup dish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are various theories on how the dish came to be named "ramen", but the most plausible is that the term was misapplied by Japanese colonizers. After the end of World War II in 1945, millions of Japanese settler colonists were repatriated to Japan from China.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They may have labeled the southern Chinese noodle dishes in Japan "ramen", based on their superficial resemblance to lamian dishes they had encountered in northern China, particularly in the Japanese-backed puppet state of <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchukuo</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This timing aligns with the first mention of <i>ramen</i> as a dish appearing in Hatsuko Kuroda's <i>Enjoyable Home Cooking</i> (1947).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:10&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Chinese immigrants in Japan initially served a wide variety of Chinese noodle soup dishes, and referred to them by their specific names. However, they were collectively referred to as <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nankin soba</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">南京そば</span></span>; <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation"><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text"><a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> noodles</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>)</span> by the Japanese. <i>Nankinmachi</i> (Nanjing Town) was the common Japanese term for areas where Chinese people settled,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the Japanese used the term "Nankin" to describe newly imported Chinese things.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, in 1903, in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama_Chinatown" title="Yokohama Chinatown">Yokohama Chinatown</a>, then known as <i>Nankinmachi</i>, there was a Nanjing noodle restaurant<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">南京蕎麦所</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nankin soba dokoro</i></span>)</span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>The dish was renamed <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">shina soba</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">支那そば</span></span>; <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation"><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">Chinese noodles</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>)</span> in 1910 by Kan'ichi Ozaki, the founder of the first specialized ramen shop.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Japanese regarded Chinese civilization as inferior and this name change reflected broader imperialist attitudes within Japanese society towards China. The word <i>washoku</i> was used for Japanese cuisine, <i>yoshoku</i> symbolized Western cuisine, and Chinese cuisine was called <i>shina</i> <i>ryori</i>. In the decades following, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shina soba</i></span> would be the most commonly used name for ramen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:9&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the word s<i>hina</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">支那</span></span>, meaning 'China') acquired a pejorative connotation through its association with anti-Chinese racism and Japanese imperialism. The word s<i>hina</i> was replaced with <i>chūka</i> across various terms in the Japanese language. <i>Chūka</i> is derived from the Japanese reading of <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Zhōnghuá</span></i> (<span lang="zh-Hans"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%AD%E5%8D%8E" class="extiw" title="wikt:中华">中华</a></span>; <span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%AD%E8%8F%AF" class="extiw" title="wikt:中華">中華</a></span>; &#39;central beauty&#39;&#41;, an official name used by the two governments claiming sovereignty over China, the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Republic of China</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">中華民國</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Zhōnghuá Mínguó</span></i>) and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People's Republic of China</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">中华人民共和国</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Zhōnghuá Rénmín Gònghéguó</span></i>). <i>Shina ryōri</i> was changed to <i><a href="/wiki/Japanese_Chinese_cuisine" title="Japanese Chinese cuisine">chūka ryōri</a></i>, and likewise, the term <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">chūka soba</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">中華そば</span></span>; <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation"><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">Chinese noodles</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>)</span> replaced <i>shina soba</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:9&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cwiertka&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cwiertka-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Nissin_Chikin_Ramen" title="Nissin Chikin Ramen">Nissin Chikin Ramen</a>, created by <a href="/wiki/Momofuku_Ando" title="Momofuku Ando">Momofuku Ando</a>, was released in 1958, and the name <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ramen</i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ラーメン</span></span>) began to spread across the country.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cwiertka&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cwiertka-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ramen</i></span> is the most popular name, but <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">chūka soba</i></span> remains prevalent in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Takayama,_Gifu" title="Takayama, Gifu">Takayama</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The two terms can be used interchangeably, though <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">chūka soba</i></span> is also often used to refer to more "classic" styles of ramen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Origin">Origin</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Rairaiken.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Rairaiken.jpg/250px-Rairaiken.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="370" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Rairaiken.jpg/500px-Rairaiken.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1310" data-file-height="1941" /></a><figcaption><i>Rairaiken</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">来々軒</span></span>), the first ramen shop, founded in 1910 by Kan'ichi Ozaki in <a href="/wiki/Asakusa" title="Asakusa">Asakusa</a>, Tokyo. The signs advertise "Chinese <a href="/wiki/Soba" title="Soba">soba</a>" (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">支那蕎麥</span></span>) and "<a href="/wiki/Cantonese_cuisine" title="Cantonese cuisine">Cantonese cuisine</a>" (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">廣東料理</span></span>).</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ramen is a Japanese adaptation of Chinese wheat <a href="/wiki/Noodle_soup" title="Noodle soup">noodle soups</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhkworld&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhkworld-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is first recorded to have appeared in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama_Chinatown" title="Yokohama Chinatown">Yokohama Chinatown</a> in the early 20th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the dishes ancestral to ramen already existed in Japan within the Chinese community since the 1880s. Although ramen takes its name from <i><a href="/wiki/Lamian" title="Lamian">lamian</a>,</i> it did not originate from the hand-pulled lamian noodles of northern China, since the noodles used in ramen are cut, not pulled.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rather, ramen is largely derived from southern Chinese noodle dishes, particularly those from <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_cuisine" title="Cantonese cuisine">Cantonese cuisine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is reflective of Yokohama Chinatown's demographics, as the majority of Chinese settlers there were <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_people" title="Cantonese people">Cantonese</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese_people" title="Shanghainese people">Shanghainese</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/S%C5%8Dmen" title="Sōmen">Sōmen</a> is another type of noodle of Chinese origin made from wheat flour, but in Japan it is distinguished from the noodles used in ramen. The noodles used for ramen today are called <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">chūkamen</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">中華麺</span></span>; <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">Chinese noodles</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>)</span> and are made with <i>kansui</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鹹水</span></span>, alkaline salt water).
</p><p>The official diary of <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dkoku-ji" title="Shōkoku-ji">Shōkoku-ji</a> Temple in Kyoto, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Inryōken Nichiroku</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">蔭涼軒日録</span></span>)</span>, mentions eating <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">jīngdàimiàn</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">経帯麪</span></span>)</span>, noodles with <i>kansui</i>, in 1488.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Okumura&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okumura-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Jīngdàimiàn</i> is the noodle of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>. This is the earliest record of <i>kansui</i> noodles being eaten in Japan.
</p><p>One theory says that ramen was introduced to Japan during the 1660s by the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">neo-Confucian</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Shunsui" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhu Shunsui">Zhu Shunsui</a>, who served as an advisor to <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Mitsukuni" title="Tokugawa Mitsukuni">Tokugawa Mitsukuni</a> after he became a refugee in Japan to escape <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Manchu rule</a>. Mitsukuni became the first Japanese person to eat ramen. However, the noodles Mitsukuni ate were a combination of starch made from <a href="/wiki/Lotus_root" class="mw-redirect" title="Lotus root">lotus root</a> and wheat flour, which is different from <i>chūkamen</i> with <i>kansui</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Okumura&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okumura-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to historians, the more plausible theory is that ramen was introduced to Japan in the late 19th<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-yrm&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yrm-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> century by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Japan" title="Chinese people in Japan">Chinese immigrants</a> living in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama_Chinatown" title="Yokohama Chinatown">Yokohama Chinatown</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1884, Chinese noodle soups had grown popular in Yokohama, <a href="/wiki/Kobe" title="Kobe">Kobe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hakodate" title="Hakodate">Hakodate</a>, however, this popularity was mostly concentrated among Chinese immigrants. The Chinese served a variety of noodle soup dishes and referred to them by their specific names, such as <i>char siu tang mian</i> (roast pork noodle soup) and <i>rousi tang mian</i> (sliced pork noodle soup)<i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></i> The Japanese referred to all these noodle soup dishes as <i>Nankin soba ('<a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> noodles')</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These noodle soups were particularly in high demand among Chinese students, who missed the cuisine of their homelands and found Japanese food bland in comparison.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>The Japanese government passed a law in 1899 allowing resident aliens to own businesses outside their designated settlements. This development, in addition to increased labor demands, led to a spread of Chinese immigrants throughout Japan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1900, restaurants serving Chinese cuisine from <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> and Shanghai offered a simple dish of noodles, a few toppings, and a broth flavored with salt and pork bones. Many Chinese living in Japan also pulled portable food stalls, selling ramen. By the mid-1900s, these stalls used a type of a musical horn called a <i><a href="/wiki/Charumera" class="mw-redirect" title="Charumera">charumera</a></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">チャルメラ</span></span>, from the Portuguese <span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">charamela</i></span>) to advertise their presence, a practice some vendors still retain via a loudspeaker and a looped recording.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="First_store">First store</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First store"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Japanese-style_Chinese_noodle_RAMEN_of_the_restaurant_RAIRAIKEN_at_Yutenji_Tokyo.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Japanese-style_Chinese_noodle_RAMEN_of_the_restaurant_RAIRAIKEN_at_Yutenji_Tokyo.jpg/250px-The_Japanese-style_Chinese_noodle_RAMEN_of_the_restaurant_RAIRAIKEN_at_Yutenji_Tokyo.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="221" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Japanese-style_Chinese_noodle_RAMEN_of_the_restaurant_RAIRAIKEN_at_Yutenji_Tokyo.jpg/500px-The_Japanese-style_Chinese_noodle_RAMEN_of_the_restaurant_RAIRAIKEN_at_Yutenji_Tokyo.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1687" data-file-height="1488" /></a><figcaption>A bowl of ramen from the second <i>Rairaiken</i> in Yūtenji, opened in 1933 by Fu Xinglei (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">傅興雷</span></span>), one of the twelve Chinese cooks from the first <i>Rairaiken</i> store in Asakusa</figcaption></figure>
<p>According to ramen expert Hiroshi Osaki, the first specialized ramen shop was <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rairaiken&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rairaiken (page does not exist)">Rairaiken</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%9D%A5%E3%80%85%E8%BB%92" class="extiw" title="ja:来々軒">ja</a>&#93;</span></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">来々軒</span></span>), which opened in 1910 in <a href="/wiki/Asakusa" title="Asakusa">Asakusa</a>, Tokyo. The Japanese founder, Kan'ichi Ozaki (尾崎貫一), employed twelve <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_people" title="Cantonese people">Cantonese</a> cooks from <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Chinatown" title="Chinatown">Chinatown</a> and served the ramen arranged for Japanese customers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-hakutukan2&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hakutukan2-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In contrast to most Japanese, who held prejudiced views toward <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cuisine" title="Chinese cuisine">Chinese cuisine</a>, Ozaki grew up in Yokohama, where he experienced Chinese food firsthand and witnessed the popularity of noodle dishes in the city's Chinatown.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Early versions of ramen were wheat noodles in broth topped with <a href="/wiki/Char_siu" title="Char siu">char siu</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ozaki changed the name of the noodle dishes from <i>Nankin soba</i> to <i>Shina soba</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The store also served standard Cantonese fare like <a href="/wiki/Wontons" class="mw-redirect" title="Wontons">wontons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shumai" title="Shumai">shumai</a>, and is sometimes regarded as the origin of Japanese-Chinese fusion dishes like <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%ABkadon" title="Chūkadon">chūkadon</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tenshindon" title="Tenshindon">tenshindon</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  
</p><p><i>Rairaiken'</i>s original store closed in 1976, but related stores with the same name currently exist in other places, and have connections to the first store.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1925, a Chinese traveller named Fan Qinxing from <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> province opened a ramen shop called <i>Genraiken</i> in <a href="/wiki/Kitakata,_Fukushima" title="Kitakata, Fukushima">Kitakata</a> as an homage to the popular <i>Rairaiken</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1933, Fu Xinglei (傅興雷), one of the twelve original chefs, opened a second <i>Rairaiken</i> in Yūtenji, <a href="/wiki/Meguro" title="Meguro">Meguro Ward</a>, Tokyo.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ong&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ong-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1968, one of Kan'ichi Ozaki's apprentices opened a store named <i>Shinraiken</i> ("New Raiken") in <a href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" title="Chiba Prefecture">Chiba Prefecture</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ong&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ong-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, Ozaki's grandson and great-great-grandson re-opened the original <i>Rairaiken</i> as a store inside <a href="/wiki/Shin-Yokohama_R%C4%81men_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Shin-Yokohama Rāmen Museum">Shin-Yokohama Rāmen Museum</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Popularization_and_modernization">Popularization and modernization</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Popularization and modernization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Shina_soba_yatai.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Shina_soba_yatai.jpg/250px-Shina_soba_yatai.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="277" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Shina_soba_yatai.jpg/500px-Shina_soba_yatai.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="854" data-file-height="947" /></a><figcaption>Women eating <i>shina soba</i> at a <i>shina soba</i> stall, Tokyo, 1956</figcaption></figure>
<p>After Japan's defeat in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/USFJ" class="mw-redirect" title="USFJ">American military</a> <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">occupied</a> the country from 1945 to 1952.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In December 1945, Japan recorded its worst rice harvest in 42 years,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which caused food shortages as Japan had drastically reduced rice production during the war as production shifted to colonies in China and Formosa island.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The US flooded the market with cheap wheat flour to deal with food shortages.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>During the same period, millions of Japanese colonizers returned from China and other parts of East Asia. It was only in 1947, in the post-war period, that the term <i>ramen</i> was first recorded in Japan to refer to the southern Chinese noodle dish that originated in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama_Chinatown" title="Yokohama Chinatown">Yokohama Chinatown</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:10&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> possibly because it superficially resembled the <a href="/wiki/Lamian" title="Lamian">lamian</a> dishes they had encountered in northern China. Many Japanese repatriates were familiar with Chinese cuisine and opened <i><a href="/wiki/Yatai_(food_cart)" title="Yatai (food cart)">yatai</a></i> (food stalls) selling ramen. <a href="/wiki/Jiaozi" title="Jiaozi">Jiaozi</a>, a staple food of northern China, also began to be served as a complement to ramen at these stalls.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These jiaozi were called <i>gyoza</i> by the Japanese, a name likely adopted in the puppet state of <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchukuo</a> and derived from the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">Manchu</a> word <i>giyose</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>From 1948 to 1951, bread consumption in Japan increased from 262,121 tons to 611,784 tons,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but wheat also found its way into ramen, which most Japanese ate at <a href="/wiki/Black_market" title="Black market">black market</a> food vendors to survive as the government food distribution system ran about 20 days behind schedule.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although the Americans maintained Japan's wartime ban on outdoor food vending,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> flour was secretly diverted from commercial mills into the black markets,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where nearly 90 percent of stalls were under the control of gangsters related to the <i><a href="/wiki/Yakuza" title="Yakuza">yakuza</a></i> who extorted vendors for protection money.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thousands of ramen vendors were arrested during the occupation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Shinjuku_(6969833313).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Shinjuku_%286969833313%29.jpg/250px-Shinjuku_%286969833313%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Shinjuku_%286969833313%29.jpg/500px-Shinjuku_%286969833313%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3535" data-file-height="2356" /></a><figcaption>A mobile ramen stall (<i><a href="/wiki/Yatai_(food_cart)" title="Yatai (food cart)">yatai</a></i>) in <a href="/wiki/Shinjuku" title="Shinjuku">Shinjuku</a>, Tokyo</figcaption></figure>
<p>By 1950 wheat flour exchange controls were removed and restrictions on food vending loosened, which further boosted the number of ramen vendors: private companies even rented out <i>yatai</i> starter kits consisting of noodles, toppings, bowls, and chopsticks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ramen <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yatai</i></span> provided a rare opportunity for small-scale postwar entrepreneurship.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Americans also aggressively advertised the nutritional benefits of wheat and animal protein.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The combination of these factors caused wheat noodles to gain prominence in Japan's rice-based culture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Gradually, ramen became associated with urban life.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm/250px-seek%3D1-Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="141" data-durationhint="14" data-mwtitle="Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8b/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8b/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm.1080p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="1080p.vp9.webm" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8&quot;" data-width="3840" data-height="2160" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8b/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8b/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm/Tonkotsu_ramen_-_tokyo_area_June_20_2021.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>A hot bowl of <i>tonkotsu</i> ramen in Tokyo</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1958, <a href="/wiki/Instant_noodle" class="mw-redirect" title="Instant noodle">instant noodles</a> were invented by <a href="/wiki/Momofuku_Ando" title="Momofuku Ando">Momofuku Ando</a>, the Taiwanese-Japanese founder and chairman of <a href="/wiki/Nissin_Foods" title="Nissin Foods">Nissin Foods</a>. Named the greatest Japanese <a href="/wiki/Invention" title="Invention">invention</a> of the 20th century in a Japanese poll,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> instant ramen allowed anyone to make an approximation of this dish simply by adding boiling water.
</p><p>Beginning in the 1980s, ramen became a Japanese cultural icon and was studied around the world. At the same time, local varieties of ramen were hitting the national market and could even be ordered by their regional names. A <a href="/wiki/Shin-Yokohama_Raumen_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Shin-Yokohama Raumen Museum">ramen museum</a> opened in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a> in 1994.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Today ramen is one of Japan's most popular foods, with Tokyo alone containing around 5,000 ramen shops,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ao2018&#95;21-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ao2018-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and more than 24,000 ramen shops across Japan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bbc2016&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2016-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Tsuta</i>, a ramen restaurant in Tokyo's <a href="/wiki/Sugamo" title="Sugamo">Sugamo</a> district, received a <a href="/wiki/Michelin_star" class="mw-redirect" title="Michelin star">Michelin star</a> in December 2015.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bbc2016&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2016-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Types">Types</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Types"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A wide variety of ramen exists in Japan, with geographical and vendor-specific differences even in varieties that share the same name. Usually varieties of ramen are differentiated by the type of broth and tare used. There are five components to a bowl of ramen: tare, aroma oil, broth, noodles, and toppings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Noodles">Noodles</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Noodles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fresh_ramen_noodle_001.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Fresh_ramen_noodle_001.jpg/250px-Fresh_ramen_noodle_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="182" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Fresh_ramen_noodle_001.jpg/500px-Fresh_ramen_noodle_001.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1876" data-file-height="1364" /></a><figcaption>Fresh ramen noodles</figcaption></figure>
<p>The type of noodles used in ramen are called <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">chūkamen</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">中華麺</span></span>; <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">Chinese noodles</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>)</span>, which are derived from traditional Chinese <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_noodles" title="Alkaline noodles">alkaline noodles</a> known as <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">jiǎnshuǐ miàn</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">鹼水麵</span></span>). Most <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">chūkamen</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"></span> are made from four basic ingredients: wheat flour, salt, water, and <i><a href="/wiki/Kansui" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansui">kansui</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E3%81%8B%E3%82%93%E6%B0%B4" class="extiw" title="ja:かん水">ja</a>&#93;</span></i>, derived from the Chinese <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">jiǎnshuǐ</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">鹼水</span></span>), a type of alkaline mineral water containing <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a> and usually <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a>, as well as sometimes a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acid</a>. Ramen is not to be confused with different kinds of noodle such as <a href="/wiki/Soba" title="Soba">soba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Udon" title="Udon">udon</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Somen" class="mw-redirect" title="Somen">somen</a>.
</p><p>The origin of <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">jiǎnshuǐ</i></span> isn't clear. It is said to originate in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>. Making noodles with <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">jiǎnshuǐ</i></span> lends them a yellowish hue as well as a firm texture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> But since there is no natural <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">jiǎnshuǐ</i></span> or <i>kansui</i> in Japan, it was difficult to make <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">jiǎnshuǐ miàn</i></span> or <i>chūkamen</i> before the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> (1868).
</p><p>Ramen comes in various shapes and lengths. It may be thick, thin, or even ribbon-like, as well as straight or wrinkled.
</p><p>Traditionally, ramen noodles were made by hand, but with growing popularity, many ramen restaurants prefer to use noodle-making machines to meet the increased demand and improve quality. Automatic ramen-making machines imitating manual production methods have been available since the mid-20th century produced by such Japanese manufacturers as Yamato MFG. and others.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Soup">Soup</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Soup"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ichiran_ramen_by_SkyChen_in_Shibuya,_Tokyo.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ichiran_ramen_by_SkyChen_in_Shibuya%2C_Tokyo.jpg/250px-Ichiran_ramen_by_SkyChen_in_Shibuya%2C_Tokyo.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ichiran_ramen_by_SkyChen_in_Shibuya%2C_Tokyo.jpg/500px-Ichiran_ramen_by_SkyChen_in_Shibuya%2C_Tokyo.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a><figcaption>Ramen in tonkotsu soup</figcaption></figure>
<p>Similar to Chinese soup bases, ramen soup is generally made from chicken or pork, though vegetable and fish stock is also used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MAFF&#95;10Tastes2019&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAFF_10Tastes2019-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This base stock is often combined with <a href="/wiki/Dashi" title="Dashi">dashi</a> stock components such as <a href="/wiki/Katsuobushi" title="Katsuobushi">katsuobushi</a> (<a href="/wiki/Skipjack_tuna" title="Skipjack tuna">skipjack tuna</a> flakes), <a href="/wiki/Niboshi" title="Niboshi">niboshi</a> (dried baby sardines),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MAFF&#95;10Tastes2019&#95;51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAFF_10Tastes2019-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> saba bushi (<a href="/wiki/Mackerel" title="Mackerel">mackerel</a> flakes), <a href="/wiki/Shiitake" title="Shiitake">shiitake</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kombu" title="Kombu">kombu</a> (kelp). Ramen stock is usually divided into two categories: chintan and paitan.
</p>
<ul><li><i><b>Chintan</b></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">清湯</span></span>), derived from the Chinese <i>qīngtāng</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">清湯</span>; &#39;clear soup&#39;), is a clear stock, made by simmering ingredients and frequently skimming foam and scum off the top of the pot.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chintan stocks are the most common kind, and can be made from chicken, pork, vegetables and/or niboshi.</li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Tonkotsu"></span><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><b>Paitan</b></i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">白湯</span></span>), derived from the Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Soups_in_East_Asian_culture#Chinese" title="Soups in East Asian culture">baitang</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">白湯</span>; &#39;white soup&#39;), is a broth with an opaque white colored appearance and a creamy consistency that rivals milk, melted butter or gravy (depending on the shop). Paitan stock is made by boiling pork or chicken bones at a high heat for hours at a time, allowing the bones to emulsify into the soup. The most well-known and common paitan stock is <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">豚骨</span></span>, 'pork bone'; not to be confused with <i><a href="/wiki/Tonkatsu" title="Tonkatsu">tonkatsu</a></i>). Although <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> is merely a kind of broth, some people consider <a href="/wiki/Tonkotsu_ramen" title="Tonkotsu ramen"><i>tonkotsu</i> ramen</a> (specialty of Kyushu, its birthplace) a distinct flavor category.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Davis2016&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2016-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When chicken bones are used to make a paitan stock, the resulting soup is called <i>tori paitan</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">鶏白湯</span></span>).</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tare">Tare</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tare"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Santoka_ramen.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Santoka_ramen.jpg/250px-Santoka_ramen.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Santoka_ramen.jpg/500px-Santoka_ramen.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Shio</i></span> ramen</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Gyoza_no_Ousho_20191210_125203.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gyoza_no_Ousho_20191210_125203.jpg/250px-Gyoza_no_Ousho_20191210_125203.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gyoza_no_Ousho_20191210_125203.jpg/500px-Gyoza_no_Ousho_20191210_125203.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3460" data-file-height="2668" /></a><figcaption><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Shōyu</i></span> ramen</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dosanko_Akaneri.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Dosanko_Akaneri.jpg/250px-Dosanko_Akaneri.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="195" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Dosanko_Akaneri.jpg/500px-Dosanko_Akaneri.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3388" data-file-height="2637" /></a><figcaption><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Miso</i></span> ramen</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Muroran_Curry_Ramen_Ajino-Daioh_Muroran_Honten.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Muroran_Curry_Ramen_Ajino-Daioh_Muroran_Honten.jpg/250px-Muroran_Curry_Ramen_Ajino-Daioh_Muroran_Honten.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Muroran_Curry_Ramen_Ajino-Daioh_Muroran_Honten.jpg/500px-Muroran_Curry_Ramen_Ajino-Daioh_Muroran_Honten.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1800" /></a><figcaption><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Karē</i></span> ramen</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tare_sauce" title="Tare sauce">Tare</a> is a sauce that is used to flavor the broth. The main purpose of tare is to provide salt to the broth, but tare also usually adds other flavors, such as umami. There are three main kinds of tare.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span class="anchor" id="Shio"></span><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><b>Shio</b></i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">塩</span></span>, 'salt') ramen is the oldest of the four types.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Davis2016&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2016-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This tare is made from cooking alcohols like <a href="/wiki/Mirin" title="Mirin">mirin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a> ingredients like kombu, niboshi and <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">MSG</a>, and salt. Occasionally pork bones are also used, but they are not boiled as long as they are for <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> ramen, so the soup remains light and clear. In shio ramen, <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%C4%81sh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Chāshū">chāshū</a></i> is sometimes swapped for lean chicken meatballs, and pickled plums and <i><a href="/wiki/Kamaboko" title="Kamaboko">kamaboko</a></i> (a slice of processed fish roll sometimes served as a frilly white circle with a pink or red spiral called <i><a href="/wiki/Narutomaki" title="Narutomaki">narutomaki</a></i>) are popular toppings as well. Noodle texture and thickness varies among <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shio</i></span> ramen, but they are usually straight rather than curly. <i><a href="/wiki/Hakodate" title="Hakodate">Hakodate</a> ramen</i> is a well-known version of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shio</i></span> ramen in Japan.</li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Shoyu"></span><span class="anchor" id="Shōyu"></span><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><b>Shōyu</b></i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">醤油</span></span>, '<a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>') tare is similar to shio tare, but with the addition of soy sauce, which boosts the salty and umami flavor even further. Adding a soy sauce seasoning to the serving bowl before the soup and noodles is a common preparation method for noodle soups from <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, as can be seen in the Chinese dish <i>yangchunmian</i>. Ramen usually has curly noodles rather than straight ones, although this is not always the case. It is often adorned with marinated bamboo shoots or <i><a href="/wiki/Menma" title="Menma">menma</a></i>, scallions, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ninjin</i></span> ('carrot'), <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kamaboko</i></span> ('fish cakes'), <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">nori</i></span> ('seaweed'), boiled eggs, bean sprouts or <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">black pepper</a>; occasionally the soup will also contain chili oil or Chinese spices, and some shops serve sliced beef instead of the usual <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%C4%81sh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Chāshū">chāshū</a></i>.</li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Miso"></span><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><b>Miso</b></i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">味噌</span></span>) ramen reached national prominence around 1965. This uniquely Japanese ramen, which was developed in Sapporo, Hokkaido, features a broth that combines copious <a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a> and is blended with oily chicken or fish broth – and sometimes with <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> or lard – to create a thick, nutty, slightly sweet and very hearty soup. <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Miso</i></span> ramen broth tends to have a robust, tangy flavor, so it stands up to a variety of flavorful toppings: spicy bean paste or <i><a href="/wiki/Doubanjiang" title="Doubanjiang">tōbanjan</a></i> (<a href="/w/index.php?title=%E8%B1%86%E7%93%A3%E9%86%A4&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="豆瓣醤 (page does not exist)">豆瓣醤</a><sup class="noprint" style="font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E8%B1%86%E7%93%A3%E9%85%B1" class="extiw" title="zh:豆瓣酱">zh</a>&#93;</sup>), butter and corn, leeks, onions, bean sprouts, ground pork, cabbage, <a href="/wiki/Sesame_seed" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesame seed">sesame seeds</a>, white pepper, chilli and chopped garlic are common. The noodles are typically thick, curly, and slightly chewy.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Toppings">Toppings</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Toppings"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:GOMOKU_SOBA_%E4%BA%94%E7%9B%AE%E3%81%9D%E3%81%B0.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/GOMOKU_SOBA_%E4%BA%94%E7%9B%AE%E3%81%9D%E3%81%B0.jpg/250px-GOMOKU_SOBA_%E4%BA%94%E7%9B%AE%E3%81%9D%E3%81%B0.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/GOMOKU_SOBA_%E4%BA%94%E7%9B%AE%E3%81%9D%E3%81%B0.jpg/500px-GOMOKU_SOBA_%E4%BA%94%E7%9B%AE%E3%81%9D%E3%81%B0.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gomoku</i></span> ramen, sometimes called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gomoku soba</i></span></figcaption></figure>
<p>After basic preparation, ramen can be adorned with any number of toppings, including but not limited to:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Ch%C4%81sh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Chāshū">Chāshū</a></i> (sliced roasted or <a href="/wiki/Red_cooking" title="Red cooking">red cooked</a> pork)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Allium_fistulosum" title="Allium fistulosum">Negi</a></i> (green onion)</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Takana-zuke</i></span> (pickled and seasoned mustard leaves)</li>
<li>Seasoned (usually <a href="/wiki/Salted_duck_egg" title="Salted duck egg">salted</a>) <a href="/wiki/Boiled_egg" title="Boiled egg">boiled egg</a> (<a href="/wiki/Soy_egg" title="Soy egg">soy egg</a>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ajitsuke tamago</i></span> or <i>ajitama</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Niku_Soboro&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Niku Soboro (page does not exist)">Niku Soboro</a></i>:Niku soboro is ground meat (this can be chicken, pork, or beef) seasoned with flavorings of soy sauce and or a little sugar and then fried... ( is more unique/contemporary)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bean_sprout" class="mw-redirect" title="Bean sprout">Bean</a> or other sprouts</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Menma" title="Menma">Menma</a></i> (Chinese lacto-fermented bamboo shoots called <a href="/wiki/Sunsi" title="Sunsi">sunsi</a>), formerly known as <i>shinachiku</i> in Japan</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kakuni" title="Kakuni">Kakuni</a></i> (braised pork cubes or squares)</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kikurage</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Black_fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Black fungi">wood ear mushroom</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nori" title="Nori">Nori</a> (dried <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kamaboko" title="Kamaboko">Kamaboko</a></i> (formed fish paste, often in a pink and white spiral called <i><a href="/wiki/Narutomaki" title="Narutomaki">narutomaki</a></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Squid_as_food" title="Squid as food">Squid</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Umeboshi" title="Umeboshi">Umeboshi</a></i> (pickled plum)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">Corn</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Butter" title="Butter">Butter</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wakame" title="Wakame">Wakame</a> (a type of seaweed)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">Olive oil</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sesame_oil" title="Sesame oil">Sesame oil</a></li>
<li><i>Mayu</i> (black garlic oil)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chili_crisp" title="Chili crisp">Chili crisp</a></li>
<li>Other types of <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Preference">Preference</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Preference"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Seasonings commonly added to ramen are white pepper, <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">black pepper</a>, butter, <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili pepper</a>, sesame seeds, and crushed garlic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Soup recipes and methods of preparation tend to be closely guarded secrets.
</p><p>Most <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> ramen restaurants offer a system known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kae-dama</i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">替え玉</span></span>), where customers who have finished their noodles can request a "refill" (for a few hundred yen more) to be put into their remaining soup.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Regional_variations">Regional variations</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Regional variations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>While standard versions of ramen are available throughout Japan since the <a href="/wiki/Taish%C5%8D_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Taishō period">Taishō period</a>, the last few decades have shown a proliferation of regional variations, commonly referred to as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">gotouchi</i></span> <i>ramen</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ご当地ラーメン</span></span> "regional ramen"). Some of these which have gone on to national prominence are:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Sapporo" title="Sapporo">Sapporo</a></b>, the capital of <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, is especially famous for its ramen. Most people in Japan associate Sapporo with its rich <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">miso</i></span> ramen, which was invented there and which is ideal for Hokkaido's harsh, snowy winters.
