Sapporo <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">miso</i></span> ramen is typically topped with sweetcorn, butter, bean sprouts, finely chopped pork, and garlic, and sometimes local seafood such as <a href="/wiki/Scallop" title="Scallop">scallop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, and crab. <a href="/wiki/Hakodate" title="Hakodate">Hakodate</a>, another city of Hokkaido, is famous for its salt-flavored ramen,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Asahikawa" title="Asahikawa">Asahikawa</a> in the north of the island offers a soy sauce-flavored variation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-a&#95;ramen&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a_ramen-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Muroran" title="Muroran">Muroran</a>, many ramen restaurants offer <a href="/wiki/Muroran_curry_ramen" title="Muroran curry ramen">Muroran curry ramen</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Kitakata_ramen" title="Kitakata ramen">Kitakata ramen</a></b> is known for its rather thick, flat, curly noodles served in a pork-and-<i><a href="/wiki/Niboshi" title="Niboshi">niboshi</a></i> broth. The area within the former city limits has the highest per-capita number of ramen establishments. Ramen has such prominence in the region that locally, the word <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">soba</i></span> usually refers to ramen, and not to actual <a href="/wiki/Soba" title="Soba">soba</a> which is referred to as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">nihon soba</i></span> ('Japanese soba').</li>
<li><b>Tokyo</b>-style ramen consists of slightly thin, curly noodles served in a soy-flavored chicken broth. The Tokyo-style broth typically has a touch of <i><a href="/wiki/Dashi" title="Dashi">dashi</a></i>, as old ramen establishments in Tokyo often originate from <a href="/wiki/Soba" title="Soba">soba</a> eateries. Standard toppings are chopped scallion, <a href="/wiki/Menma" title="Menma">menma</a>, sliced pork, kamaboko, egg, nori, and spinach. <a href="/wiki/Ikebukuro" title="Ikebukuro">Ikebukuro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ogikubo" class="mw-redirect" title="Ogikubo">Ogikubo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ebisu,_Shibuya" title="Ebisu, Shibuya">Ebisu</a> are three areas in Tokyo known for their ramen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a></b> ramen specialty is called <a href="/wiki/Iekei_Ramen" class="mw-redirect" title="Iekei Ramen">Ie-kei</a> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">家系</span></span>). It consists of thick, straight noodles served in a soy flavored pork broth similar to <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span>, sometimes referred to as, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu-shoyu</i></span>. The standard toppings are braised pork (<a href="/wiki/Ch%C4%81sh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Chāshū">chāshū</a>), boiled spinach, sheets of nori, often with shredded <a href="/wiki/Welsh_onion" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh onion">Welsh onion</a> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">negi</i></span>) and a soft- or hard-boiled egg. It is traditional for customers to customize the softness of the noodles, the richness of the broth and the amount of oil they want.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wakayama_Prefecture" title="Wakayama Prefecture">Wakayama</a></b> ramen in the <a href="/wiki/Kansai_region" title="Kansai region">Kansai region</a> has a broth made from soy sauce and pork bones.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Hakata_ramen" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakata ramen">Hakata ramen</a></b> originates from <a href="/wiki/Hakata-ku,_Fukuoka" title="Hakata-ku, Fukuoka">Hakata</a> district of <a href="/wiki/Fukuoka" title="Fukuoka">Fukuoka</a> city in <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>. It has a rich, milky, pork-bone <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> broth and rather thin, non-curly and resilient noodles. Often, distinctive toppings such as crushed garlic, <i><a href="/wiki/Beni_sh%C5%8Dga" title="Beni shōga">beni shōga</a></i> (pickled ginger), sesame seeds, and spicy <a href="/wiki/Tsukemono" title="Tsukemono">pickled</a> mustard greens (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">karashi takana</i></span>) are left on tables for customers to serve themselves. Ramen <a href="/wiki/Yatai_(retail)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yatai (retail)">stalls</a> in Hakata and <a href="/wiki/Tenjin,_Fukuoka" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenjin, Fukuoka">Tenjin</a> are well known within Japan. Recent trends have made Hakata ramen one of the most popular types in Japan, and several chain restaurants specializing in Hakata ramen can be found all over the country.</li>
<li>Tofu ramen is a specialty of <a href="/wiki/Iwatsuki-ku,_Saitama" title="Iwatsuki-ku, Saitama">Iwatsuki ward</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saitama_(city)" title="Saitama (city)">Saitama City</a>.</li>
<li><b>Nabeyaki</b> ramen is a specialty of <a href="/wiki/Susaki,_K%C5%8Dchi" title="Susaki, Kōchi">Susaki City</a>, as well as other cities in western <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dchi_Prefecture" title="Kōchi Prefecture">Kōchi Prefecture</a>. Nabeyaki ramen is made with a chicken-based broth, thin noodles and a soy tare, all served boiling hot in an enamelled pot. Toppings vary, but mainstays include a raw egg that poaches in the bowl, sliced spring onions and <a href="/wiki/Chikuwa" title="Chikuwa">chikuwa</a> fish cakes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Nagoya" title="Nagoya">Nagoya</a></b> ramen specialties include "Taiwan ramen", which despite its name originated in Nagoya and features a very spicy broth. It became famous in the 1980s during a fad for super hot food. It bears some resemblance to <a href="/wiki/Danzai_noodles" class="mw-redirect" title="Danzai noodles">danzai noodles</a> but has both a spicy broth and spicy minced meat resulting in an extremely spicy dish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Related_dishes">Related dishes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Related dishes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are many related, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cuisine" title="Chinese cuisine">Chinese-influenced</a> noodle dishes in Japan. The following are often served alongside ramen in ramen establishments. They do not include noodle dishes considered traditionally Japanese, such as <a href="/wiki/Soba" title="Soba">soba</a> or <a href="/wiki/Udon" title="Udon">udon</a>, which are almost never served in the same establishments as ramen.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nagasaki,_Nagasaki" class="mw-redirect" title="Nagasaki, Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Champon" title="Champon">champon</a></i>. Japanese version of <a href="/wiki/Fujian_cuisine" title="Fujian cuisine">Fujianese</a> <i>menmian</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">焖面</span>). The noodles are thicker than ramen but thinner than udon. <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Champon</i></span> is topped with a variety of ingredients, mostly seafood, stir-fried and dressed in a starchy sauce. The stir-fried ingredients are poured directly over the cooked noodles, with the sauce acting as a soup.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tan-men</i></span> derived is a mild, usually salty soup, served with a mix of sautéed vegetables and seafood/pork. The name is derived from the generic Chinese term for any wheat noodle soup (<span lang="zh-Hans">汤面</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">tāngmiàn</span></i>). The origins of tanmen are attributed to Japanese chefs who repatriated from the puppet state of Manchukuo after World War II and sought to recreate the flavors of the Chinese home-style cooking they had encountered.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Not to be confused with <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tantan-men</i></span> (see after).</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Wantan-men</i></span>. Japanese version of <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_cuisine" title="Cantonese cuisine">Cantonese</a> <a href="/wiki/Wonton_noodles" title="Wonton noodles">wonton noodles</a>. It has long, straight noodles and <a href="/wiki/Wonton" title="Wonton">wonton</a>, served in a mild, usually salty soup.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Tsukemen" title="Tsukemen">Tsukemen</a></i> ('dipping noodles'). The noodles and soup are served in separate bowls. The diner dips the noodles in the soup before eating. Can be served hot or chilled.</li></ul>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Soupless_Dandan_noodles_Hiroshima_Type_%E5%BA%83%E5%B3%B6%E5%BC%8F_%E6%B1%81%E3%81%AA%E3%81%97_%E6%8B%85%E6%8B%85%E9%BA%BA.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Soupless_Dandan_noodles_Hiroshima_Type_%E5%BA%83%E5%B3%B6%E5%BC%8F_%E6%B1%81%E3%81%AA%E3%81%97_%E6%8B%85%E6%8B%85%E9%BA%BA.jpg/250px-Soupless_Dandan_noodles_Hiroshima_Type_%E5%BA%83%E5%B3%B6%E5%BC%8F_%E6%B1%81%E3%81%AA%E3%81%97_%E6%8B%85%E6%8B%85%E9%BA%BA.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Soupless_Dandan_noodles_Hiroshima_Type_%E5%BA%83%E5%B3%B6%E5%BC%8F_%E6%B1%81%E3%81%AA%E3%81%97_%E6%8B%85%E6%8B%85%E9%BA%BA.jpg/500px-Soupless_Dandan_noodles_Hiroshima_Type_%E5%BA%83%E5%B3%B6%E5%BC%8F_%E6%B1%81%E3%81%AA%E3%81%97_%E6%8B%85%E6%8B%85%E9%BA%BA.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption>Hiroshma-type <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tantan-men</i></span>, or soupless dandan noodle</figcaption></figure>
<ul><li><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tantan-men</i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">担担麺</span></span>). Japanese version of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan_cuisine" title="Sichuan cuisine">Sichuanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Dan_dan_noodles" class="mw-redirect" title="Dan dan noodles">dan dan noodles</a>. Ramen in a reddish, spicy chili and sesame soup, usually containing minced pork, garnished with chopped <a href="/wiki/Scallion" title="Scallion">scallion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili</a> and occasionally topped with spinach or bok choi (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">chingensai</i></span>).</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Sūrātanmen</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sanrātanmen</i></span> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">酸辣湯麺</span></span>, 'noodles in <a href="/wiki/Hot_and_sour_soup" title="Hot and sour soup">hot and sour soup</a>'). Japanese version of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan_cuisine" title="Sichuan cuisine">Sichuanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Hot_and_sour_soup" title="Hot and sour soup">hot and sour soup</a>, but served with long noodles. The topping ingredients are sautéed and a thickener is added before the mix is poured on the soup and the noodles.</li>
<li><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Aburasoba" title="Aburasoba">Aburasoba</a></i></span> ('oil-noodles'). Ramen and toppings served without the soup, but with a small quantity of oily soy-based sauce instead.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hiyashi_ch%C5%ABka" title="Hiyashi chūka">Hiyashi-chūka</a></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">冷やし中華</span></span>, 'chilled Chinese'). Japanese version of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_cuisine" title="Shanghai cuisine">Shanghainese</a> <i>liangbanmian</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">凉拌面</span>). The dish was originally sold in Japan under the borrowed Chinese name <i>ryanbanmyen</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is a  summer dish of chilled ramen on a plate with various toppings (typically thin strips of <a href="/wiki/Omelet" class="mw-redirect" title="Omelet">omelet</a>, ham, cucumber and tomato) and served with a vinegary soy dressing and <i><a href="/wiki/Karashi" title="Karashi">karashi</a></i> (Japanese mustard). It was first produced at the Ryutei, a Chinese restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Sendai#Specialties_and_crafts" title="Sendai">Sendai</a>. It is also known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">reimen</i></span>, especially in western Japan.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Restaurants_in_Japan">Restaurants in Japan</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Restaurants in Japan"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ichiran_in_Shinjuku_Kabukicho_2018.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ichiran_in_Shinjuku_Kabukicho_2018.jpg/250px-Ichiran_in_Shinjuku_Kabukicho_2018.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ichiran_in_Shinjuku_Kabukicho_2018.jpg/500px-Ichiran_in_Shinjuku_Kabukicho_2018.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="1824" /></a><figcaption>An <a href="/wiki/Ichiran" title="Ichiran">Ichiran</a> ramen restaurant in Shinjuku Kabukicho</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ramen is offered in various types of restaurants and locations including ramen shops, <i><a href="/wiki/Izakaya" title="Izakaya">izakaya</a></i> drinking establishments, lunch cafeterias, <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">karaoke</a> halls, and amusement parks. Many ramen restaurants only have a counter and a chef. In these shops, the meals are paid for in advance at a ticket machine to streamline the process.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some restaurants also provide <i><a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a></i> ramen (using chicken) in Osaka and Kyoto.
</p><p>However, the best quality ramen is usually only available in specialist <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ramen-ya</i></span> restaurants. As <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ramen-ya</i></span> restaurants offer mainly ramen dishes, they tend to lack variety in the menu. Besides ramen, some of the dishes generally available in a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ramen-ya</i></span> restaurant include other dishes from <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Chinese_cuisine" title="Japanese Chinese cuisine">Japanese Chinese cuisine</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Fried_rice" title="Fried rice">fried rice</a> (called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">chahan</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yakimeshi</i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Jiaozi" title="Jiaozi">jiaozi</a> (called <i>gyoza</i>), and alcohol. <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ramen-ya</i></span> often feature Chinese-inspired decorations. The bowls used to serve ramen may be designed to include Chinese motifs such as <a href="/wiki/Xiangyun_(Auspicious_clouds)#Yunleiwen/_Yunwen/_Leiwen" title="Xiangyun (Auspicious clouds)">yunleiwen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">loong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fenghuang" title="Fenghuang">fenghuang</a>, and the character for <a href="/wiki/Double_Happiness_(calligraphy)" title="Double Happiness (calligraphy)">double happiness</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_spoon" title="Chinese spoon">Chinese spoons</a> are more commonly used to drink the soup in ramen, as opposed to the Japanese ladle (otamajakushi), which is typically used for soba and udon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>From January 2020 to September 2021 during the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>, many ramen restaurants were temporarily closed, with 34 chains filing for bankruptcy by September 2020. Ramen restaurants are typically narrow and seat customers closely, making <a href="/wiki/Social_distancing" title="Social distancing">social distancing</a> difficult.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-returnramen&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-returnramen-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Outside_Japan">Outside Japan</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Outside Japan"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Ramen became popular in China where it is known as <span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn">rìshì lāmiàn</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">日式拉麵</span></span>, <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">Japanese-style <a href="/wiki/Lamian" title="Lamian">lamian</a></span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>). Restaurant chains serve ramen alongside  <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese dishes</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Tempura" title="Tempura">tempura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yakitori" title="Yakitori">yakitori</a>. In Japan, these dishes are not traditionally served with ramen, but <a href="/wiki/Gyoza" class="mw-redirect" title="Gyoza">gyoza</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kara-age" class="mw-redirect" title="Kara-age">kara-age</a></i>, and others from <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Chinese_cuisine" title="Japanese Chinese cuisine">Japanese Chinese cuisine</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Ramyeon"></span>In Korea, there is a variation of ramen called <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_instant_noodle_brands#South_Korea" title="List of instant noodle brands">ramyeon</a></i> (<span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hang">라면</span></span>&#59; <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hani">拉麵</span></span>), made much spicier than ramen. There are different varieties, such as <a href="/wiki/Kimchi" title="Kimchi">kimchi</a>-flavored <span title="Korean-language text"><i lang="ko-Latn">ramyeon</i></span>. While usually served with egg or vegetables such as carrots and scallions, some restaurants serve variations of <span title="Korean-language text"><i lang="ko-Latn">ramyeon</i></span> containing additional ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Mandu_(food)" title="Mandu (food)">dumplings</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tteok" title="Tteok">tteok</a></i>, or cheese as toppings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Famous ramyeon brands include <a href="/wiki/Shin_Ramyun" title="Shin Ramyun">Shin Ramyeon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buldak_Ramen" title="Buldak Ramen">Buldak Ramyeon</a>.
</p><p>Outside of Asia, particularly in areas with a large demand for <a href="/wiki/Asian_cuisine" title="Asian cuisine">Asian cuisine</a>, there are restaurants specializing in Japanese-style foods such as ramen noodles. For example, <a href="/wiki/Wagamama" title="Wagamama">Wagamama</a>, a UK-based restaurant chain serving pan-Asian food, serves a ramen noodle soup and in the United States and Canada, <a href="/wiki/Jinya_Ramen_Bar" title="Jinya Ramen Bar">Jinya Ramen Bar</a> serves <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> ramen.
</p><p>Chef Antonio de Livier of <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a> is credited with a Mexican variation called <i>birriamen</i> (a portmanteau of <a href="/wiki/Birria" title="Birria">birria</a> and ramen) where the broth incorporates the consomé broth that the beef, lamb or goat is cooked in. The variation later gained popularity in the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> area.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Instant_ramen">Instant ramen</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Instant ramen"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Instant_noodles" title="Instant noodles">Instant noodles</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cup_noodles_by_hirotomo_at_Mount_Fuji.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Cup_noodles_by_hirotomo_at_Mount_Fuji.jpg/250px-Cup_noodles_by_hirotomo_at_Mount_Fuji.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Cup_noodles_by_hirotomo_at_Mount_Fuji.jpg/500px-Cup_noodles_by_hirotomo_at_Mount_Fuji.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a><figcaption>Instant ramen in Japan</figcaption></figure>
<p>Instant ramen noodles were exported from Japan by <a href="/wiki/Nissin_Foods" title="Nissin Foods">Nissin Foods</a> starting in 1971, bearing the name "Oodles of Noodles".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One year later, it was re-branded "Nissin <a href="/wiki/Cup_Noodles" title="Cup Noodles">Cup Noodles</a>", packaged in a <a href="/wiki/Foam_food_container" title="Foam food container">foam food container</a> (It is referred to as <b>Cup Ramen</b> in Japan), and subsequently saw a growth in international sales. Over time, the term <i>ramen</i> became used in North America to refer to other instant noodles.
</p><p>While some research has claimed that consuming instant ramen two or more times a week increases the likelihood of developing heart disease and other conditions, including diabetes and stroke, especially in women, those claims have not been reproduced and no study has isolated instant ramen consumption as an aggravating factor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, instant ramen noodles, known to have a serving of 43 g, consist of very high sodium.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At least 1,760&#160;mg of sodium are found in one packet alone. It consists of 385 kilocalories, 55.7 g of carbohydrates, 14.5 g of total fat, 6.5 g of saturated fat, 7.9 g of protein, and 0.6&#160;mg of thiamine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Questionable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (December 2022)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Canned_version">Canned version</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Canned version"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Akihabara" title="Akihabara">Akihabara</a>, Tokyo, vending machines distribute warm ramen in a steel can known as <i>ramen kan</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">らーめん缶</span></span>)</span>. It is produced by a popular local ramen restaurant in flavors such as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tonkotsu</i></span> and curry, and contains noodles, soup, <i><a href="/wiki/Menma" title="Menma">menma</a></i>, and pork. It is intended as a quick snack, and includes a small folded plastic fork.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Emoji">Emoji</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Emoji"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In October 2010, an <a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">emoji</a> was approved for <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> 6.0 <span class="nowrap"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049734">.mw-parser-output .monospaced{font-family:monospace,monospace}</style><span class="monospaced">U+1F35C</span>&#x20;</span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">&#x1f35c;</span> <span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase; font-feature-settings: &#39;onum&#39;">STEAMING BOWL</span> for "Steaming Bowl", that depicts Japanese ramen noodles in a bowl of steaming broth with chopsticks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2015, the icon was added to Emoji 1.0.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Film">Film</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Film"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The main storyline of <i><a href="/wiki/Tampopo" title="Tampopo">Tampopo</a></i>, a 1985 Japanese comedy billed as the first "ramen western" (a play on the "<a href="/wiki/Spaghetti_Western" title="Spaghetti Western">spaghetti Western</a>" subgenre), concerns a trucker helping a widowed ramen shop owner reach the top of her craft.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Museum">Museum</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ramen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Museum"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shin-Yokohama_R%C4%81men_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Shin-Yokohama Rāmen Museum">Shin-Yokohama Rāmen Museum</a> is a museum about ramen, in the <a href="/wiki/Shin-Yokohama" title="Shin-Yokohama">Shin-Yokohama</a> district of <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dhoku-ku,_Yokohama" title="Kōhoku-ku, Yokohama">Kōhoku-ku, Yokohama</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Shinyokohama_ramen_museum.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Shinyokohama_ramen_museum.jpg/250px-Shinyokohama_ramen_museum.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Shinyokohama_ramen_museum.jpg/500px-Shinyokohama_ramen_museum.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a><figcaption>Shin-Yokohama Rāmen Museum</figcaption></figure>

<li id="cite&#95;note-:9-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:9_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:9_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFSugino2024" class="citation journal cs1">Sugino, Corinne Mitsuye (2024). <span class="id-lock-subscription" title="Paid subscription required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://muse.jhu.edu/pub/1/article/953150">"A Critical Culinary Genealogy of Japanese Foodways"</a></span>. <i>Journal of Asian American Studies</i>. <b>27</b> (3): <span class="nowrap">343–</span>371. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1353%2Fjaas.2024.a953150">10.1353/jaas.2024.a953150</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/issn/1096-8598">1096-8598</a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Asian+American+Studies&amp;rft.atitle=A+Critical+Culinary+Genealogy+of+Japanese+Foodways&amp;rft.volume=27&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=343-371&amp;rft.date=2024&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1353%2Fjaas.2024.a953150&amp;rft.issn=1096-8598&amp;rft.aulast=Sugino&amp;rft.aufirst=Corinne+Mitsuye&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fmuse.jhu.edu%2Fpub%2F1%2Farticle%2F953150&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARamen" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-Cwiertka-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Cwiertka_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cwiertka_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFCwiertka2006" class="citation book cs1">Cwiertka, Katarzyna Joanna (2006). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=oK-7LOlWNpEC&amp;pg=PA144"><i>Modern Japanese cuisine: food, power and national identity</i></a>. Reaktion Books. p.&#160;144. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-86189-298-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-86189-298-0"><bdi>978-1-86189-298-0</bdi></a>. <q>However, <i>Shina soba</i> acquired the status of 'national' dish in Japan under a different name: <i>rāmen</i>. The change of name from <i>Shina soba</i> to <i>rāmen</i> took place during the 1950s and '60s. The word <i>Shina</i>, used historically in reference to China, acquired a pejorative connotation through its association with Japanese imperialist association in Asia and was replaced with the word <i>Chūka</i>, which derived from the Chinese name for the People's Republic. For a while, the term <i>Chūka soba</i> was used, but ultimately the name <i>rāmen</i> caught on, inspired by the chicken-flavored instant version of the dish that went on sale in 1958 and spread nationwide in no time.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Modern+Japanese+cuisine%3A+food%2C+power+and+national+identity&amp;rft.pages=144&amp;rft.pub=Reaktion+Books&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-86189-298-0&amp;rft.aulast=Cwiertka&amp;rft.aufirst=Katarzyna+Joanna&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DoK-7LOlWNpEC%26pg%3DPA144&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARamen" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
